<ul><li><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="693" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_of_David.svg/14px-Star_of_David.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_of_David.svg/21px-Star_of_David.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_of_David.svg/28px-Star_of_David.svg.png 2x" width="14"/> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Judaism" title="Portal:Judaism">Judaism portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Judaism" title="Template:Judaism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Judaism" title="Template talk:Judaism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Judaism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Abraham is given a high position of respect in three major world faiths, <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>. In Judaism, he is the founding father of the covenant, the special relationship between the Jewish people and God – leading to the belief that the <a href="/wiki/Jews_as_the_chosen_people" title="Jews as the chosen people">Jews are the chosen people of God</a>. In Christianity, <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul the Apostle</a> taught that Abraham's faith in God – preceding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosaic_law" title="Mosaic law">Mosaic law</a> – made him the prototype of all believers, Jewish or <a href="/wiki/Gentile" title="Gentile">gentile</a>; and in Islam he is seen as a link in the <a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">chain of prophets</a> that begins with <a href="/wiki/Adam" title="Adam">Adam</a> and culminates in <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevenson20128_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevenson20128-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judaism">Judaism</span></h3>
<p>In Jewish tradition, Abraham is called <i>Avraham Avinu</i> (אברהם אבינו), "our father Abraham," signifying that he is both the biological progenitor of the Jews and the father of Judaism, the first Jew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevenson20123_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevenson20123-1">[1]</a></sup> His story is read in the weekly <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> reading portions, predominantly in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parashot" title="Parashot">parashot</a>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lech-Lecha_(parsha)" title="Lech-Lecha (parsha)">Lech-Lecha</a> (לֶךְ-לְךָ), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vayeira_(parsha)" title="Vayeira (parsha)">Vayeira</a> (וַיֵּרָא), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chayei_Sarah_(parsha)" title="Chayei Sarah (parsha)">Chayei Sarah</a> (חַיֵּי שָׂרָה), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toledot_(parsha)" title="Toledot (parsha)">Toledot</a> (תּוֹלְדֹת).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hanan_bar_Rava" title="Hanan bar Rava">Hanan bar Rava</a> taught in <a href="/wiki/Abba_Arikha" title="Abba Arikha">Abba Arikha</a>'s name that Abraham's mother was named ʾĂmatlaʾy bat Karnebo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[d]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hiyya_bar_Abba" title="Hiyya bar Abba">Hiyya bar Abba</a> taught that <a href="/wiki/Abraham_and_the_Idol_Shop" title="Abraham and the Idol Shop">Abraham worked in Teraḥ's idol shop</a> in his youth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Legends_of_the_Jews" title="Legends of the Jews">Legends of the Jews</a></i>, God created heaven and earth for the sake of the merits of Abraham.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGinzberg1909Vol_I:_The_Wicked_Generations_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGinzberg1909Vol_I:_The_Wicked_Generations-85">[81]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Genesis_flood_narrative" title="Genesis flood narrative">biblical flood</a>, Abraham was the only one among the pious who solemnly swore never to forsake God,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGinzberg1909Vol._I:_In_the_Fiery_Furnace_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGinzberg1909Vol._I:_In_the_Fiery_Furnace-86">[82]</a></sup> studied in the house of <a href="/wiki/Noah" title="Noah">Noah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shem" title="Shem">Shem</a> to learn about the "Ways of God,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJasher184022Ch9,_vv_5-6_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJasher184022Ch9,_vv_5-6-87">[83]</a></sup> continued the line of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kohanim" title="Kohanim">High Priest</a> from Noah and Shem, and assigning the office to <a href="/wiki/Levi" title="Levi">Levi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Levi" title="Tribe of Levi">his seed</a> forever. Before leaving his father's land, Abraham was miraculously saved from the fiery furnace of <a href="/wiki/Nimrod" title="Nimrod">Nimrod</a> following his brave action of breaking the idols of the <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">Chaldeans</a> into pieces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGinzberg1909_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGinzberg1909-88">[84]</a></sup> During his sojourning in Canaan, Abraham was accustomed to extend hospitality to travelers and strangers and taught how to praise God also knowledge of God to those who had received his kindness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGinzberg1909Vol._I:_The_Covenant_with_Abimelech_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGinzberg1909Vol._I:_The_Covenant_with_Abimelech-89">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Along with <a href="/wiki/Isaac_and_Jacob" title="Isaac and Jacob">Isaac and Jacob</a>, he is the one whose name would appear united with God, as <a href="/wiki/God_in_Judaism" title="God in Judaism">God in Judaism</a> was called <i>Elohei Abraham, Elohei Yitzchaq ve Elohei Ya'aqob</i> ("God of Abraham, God of Isaac, and God of Jacob") and never the God of anyone else.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGinzberg1909Vol._I:_Joy_and_Sorrow_in_the_House_of_Jacob_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGinzberg1909Vol._I:_Joy_and_Sorrow_in_the_House_of_Jacob-90">[86]</a></sup> He was also mentioned as the father of thirty nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGinzberg1909Vol._I:_The_Birth_of_Esau_and_Jacob_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGinzberg1909Vol._I:_The_Birth_of_Esau_and_Jacob-91">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Abraham is generally credited as the author of the <i><a href="/wiki/Sefer_Yetzirah" title="Sefer Yetzirah">Sefer Yetzirah</a></i>, one of the earliest extant books on <a href="/wiki/Jewish_mysticism" title="Jewish mysticism">Jewish mysticism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Pirkei_Avot" title="Pirkei Avot">Pirkei Avot</a>, Abraham underwent ten tests at God's command.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[89]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Binding_of_Isaac" title="Binding of Isaac">Binding of Isaac</a> is specified in the Bible as a test;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[90]</a></sup> the other nine are not specified, but later rabbinical sources give various enumerations.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox vcard"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above n" colspan="2" style="background-color:gold;"><div class="fn" style="display:inline;">Abraham</div></th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aert_de_Gelder_009.jpg"><img alt="Aert de Gelder 009.jpg" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Aert_de_Gelder_009.jpg/240px-Aert_de_Gelder_009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Aert_de_Gelder_009.jpg/360px-Aert_de_Gelder_009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Aert_de_Gelder_009.jpg/480px-Aert_de_Gelder_009.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a><div class="infobox-caption"><i>Abraham and the Angels</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Aert_de_Gelder" title="Aert de Gelder">Aert de Gelder</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1680–85</span> (<a href="/wiki/Museum_Boijmans_Van_Beuningen" title="Museum Boijmans Van Beuningen">Museum Boijmans Van Beuningen</a>, Rotterdam)</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color:gold;">First Patriarch</th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Venerated in</th><td class="infobox-data"><div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a></li><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Druze_faith" title="Druze faith">Druze faith</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hitti_1928_37_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hitti_1928_37-95">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dana_2008_17_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dana_2008_17-96">[92]</a></sup></li><li><a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints" title="Calendar of saints">Feast</a></th><td class="infobox-data">9 October – Roman Catholicism and Lutheranism<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LCMS_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-LCMS-97">[93]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, Abraham is revered as the <a href="/wiki/Prophets_of_Christianity" title="Prophets of Christianity">prophet</a> to whom God chose to reveal himself and with whom God initiated a <a href="/wiki/Covenant_(biblical)" title="Covenant (biblical)">covenant</a> (cf. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Covenant_Theology" title="Covenant Theology">Covenant Theology</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright201072_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright201072-9">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatersReidMuether2020_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatersReidMuether2020-98">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul the Apostle</a> declared that all who believe in Jesus (<a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a>) are "included in the seed of Abraham and are inheritors of the promise made to Abraham."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright201072_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright201072-9">[6]</a></sup> In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Letter_to_the_Romans" title="Letter to the Romans">Romans</a> 4, Abraham is praised for his "unwavering faith" in God, which is tied into the concept of partakers of the covenant of grace being those "who demonstrate faith in the saving power of Christ".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatersReidMuether2020_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatersReidMuether2020-98">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout history, church leaders, following Paul, have emphasized Abraham as the spiritual father of all Christians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJeffrey199210_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJeffrey199210-100">[96]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a> declared that Christians are "children (or "seed") of Abraham by faith", <a href="/wiki/Ambrose" title="Ambrose">Ambrose</a> stated that "by means of their faith Christians possess the promises made to Abraham", and <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> recalled Abraham as "a paradigm of the man of faith."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[e]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>, the largest Christian denomination, calls Abraham "our father in Faith" in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eucharistic_prayer" title="Eucharistic prayer">Eucharistic prayer</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Canon" title="Roman Canon">Roman Canon</a>, recited during the <a href="/wiki/Mass_(liturgy)" title="Mass (liturgy)">Mass</a>. He is also commemorated in the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints" title="Calendar of saints">calendars of saints</a> of several denominations: on 20 August by the <a href="/wiki/Maronite_Church" title="Maronite Church">Maronite Church</a>, 28 August in the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church" title="Coptic Orthodox Church">Coptic Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a> (with the full <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_office" title="Daily office">office</a> for the latter), and on 9 October by the Roman Catholic Church and the <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_Church%E2%80%93Missouri_Synod" title="Lutheran Church–Missouri Synod">Lutheran Church–Missouri Synod</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LCMS_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-LCMS-97">[93]</a></sup> In the introduction to his 15th-century translation of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Legend" title="Golden Legend">Golden Legend</a>'s account of Abraham, <a href="/wiki/William_Caxton" title="William Caxton">William Caxton</a> noted that this patriarch's life was read in church on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunday_before_Lent" title="Sunday before Lent">Quinquagesima Sunday</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caxton_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caxton-102">[97]</a></sup>
He is the <a href="/wiki/Patron_saint" title="Patron saint">patron saint</a> of those in the hospitality industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolweck1924_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolweck1924-103">[98]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2017)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> commemorates him as the "Righteous Forefather Abraham", with two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feast_day" title="Feast day">feast days</a> in its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church_liturgical_calendar" title="Eastern Orthodox Church liturgical calendar">liturgical calendar</a>. The first time is on 9 October (for those churches which follow the traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julian_Calendar" title="Julian Calendar">Julian Calendar</a>, 9 October falls on 22 October of the modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gregorian_Calendar" title="Gregorian Calendar">Gregorian Calendar</a>), where he is commemorated together with his nephew "Righteous Lot". The other is on the "Sunday of the Forefathers" (two Sundays before Christmas), when he is commemorated together with other <a href="/wiki/Genealogy_of_Jesus" title="Genealogy of Jesus">ancestors of Jesus</a>. Abraham is also mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Divine_Liturgy" title="Divine Liturgy">Divine Liturgy</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basil_the_Great" title="Basil the Great">Basil the Great</a>, just before the Anaphora, and Abraham and Sarah are invoked in the prayers said by the priest over a newly married couple.
</p><p>Some Christian theologians equate the <a href="/wiki/Abraham#Three_visitors" title="Abraham">"three visitors"</a> with the Holy <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>, seeing in their apparition a <a href="/wiki/Theophany" title="Theophany">theophany</a> experienced by Abraham<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bucur_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bucur-104">[99]</a></sup> (see also the articles on the <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantinian</a> <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilica</a> at <a href="/wiki/Mamre#Late_Roman_festival_and_Byzantine_basilica" title="Mamre">Mamre</a> and the church at the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Oak_of_Mamre" title="Oak of Mamre">Oak of Mamre</a>").
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Abraham_in_Islam" title="Abraham in Islam">Abraham in Islam</a></div>
<p>Islam regards Abraham as a link in the chain of prophets that begins with Adam and culminates in <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevenson2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidEUO2Mhd-drcCpgPA8vsnippetq22chain20of20prophets22_PA8]_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevenson2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidEUO2Mhd-drcCpgPA8vsnippetq22chain20of20prophets22_PA8]-105">[100]</a></sup>
<i>Ibrāhīm</i> is mentioned in 35 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chapters_of_the_Quran" title="Chapters of the Quran">chapters of the Quran</a>, more often than any other biblical personage apart from <a href="/wiki/Moses_in_Islam" title="Moses in Islam">Moses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeters2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidHYJ2c9E9IM8CpgPA9vonepageqthirty-five20chapters_PA9]_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeters2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidHYJ2c9E9IM8CpgPA9vonepageqthirty-five20chapters_PA9]-106">[101]</a></sup> He is called both a <i>hanif</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monotheist" title="Monotheist">monotheist</a>) and <i>muslim</i> (one who submits),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevenson2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidEUO2Mhd-drcCpgPA200vonepageqhanif20muslim_PA200]_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevenson2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidEUO2Mhd-drcCpgPA200vonepageqhanif20muslim_PA200]-107">[102]</a></sup> and Muslims regard him as a <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patriarch" title="Patriarch">patriarch</a>, the archetype of the perfect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a>, and the revered reformer of the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELings2004_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELings2004-108">[103]</a></sup> Islamic traditions consider Ibrāhīm the first Pioneer of Islam (which is also called <i>millat Ibrahim</i>, the "religion of Abraham"), and that his purpose and mission throughout his life was to proclaim the <a href="/wiki/Tawhid" title="Tawhid">Oneness of God</a>. In Islam, Abraham holds an exalted position among the major prophets and he is referred to as "Ibrahim Khalilullah", meaning "Abraham the Beloved of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a>".
</p><p>Besides <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_Isaac" title="Islamic view of Isaac">Ishaq</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yaqub" title="Yaqub">Yaqub</a>, Ibrahim is among the most honorable and the most excellent men in sight of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaulana2006104_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaulana2006104-111">[106]</a></sup> Ibrahim was also mentioned in Quran as "Father of Muslims" and the role model for the community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Druze">Druze</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a> regard Abraham as the third spokesman (<i>natiq</i>) after <a href="/wiki/Adam" title="Adam">Adam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Noah" title="Noah">Noah</a>, who helped transmit the foundational teachings of monotheism (<i>tawhid</i>) intended for the larger audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwayd20093_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwayd20093-115">[110]</a></sup> He is also among the seven prophets who appeared in different periods of history according to the Druze faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hitti_1928_37_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hitti_1928_37-95">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dana_2008_17_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dana_2008_17-96">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mandaeism">Mandaeism</span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Mandaeism" title="Mandaeism">Mandaeism</a>, Abraham (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_Mandaic_language" title="Classical Mandaic language">Classical Mandaic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="myz">ࡀࡁࡓࡀࡄࡉࡌ</span>, <small>romanized: </small><span title="Classical Mandaic-language romanization"><i lang="myz-Latn">Abrahim</i></span>) is mentioned in <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Translation:Ginza_Rabba/Right_Ginza/Book_18" title="s:Translation:Ginza Rabba/Right Ginza/Book 18">Book 18</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/Right_Ginza" title="Right Ginza">Right Ginza</a></i> as the patriarch of the Jewish people. <a href="/wiki/Mandaeans" title="Mandaeans">Mandaeans</a> consider Abraham to have been originally a Mandaean priest, however they differ with Abraham and Jews regarding circumcision which they consider to be bodily mutilation and therefore forbidden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GR_Gelbert_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-GR_Gelbert-116">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GR_Lidzbarski_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-GR_Lidzbarski-117">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DrowerHaranGawaita_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-DrowerHaranGawaita-118">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto2_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-119">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 18, 185">: 18, 185 </span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_arts">In the arts</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Painting_and_sculpture">Painting and sculpture</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Isaac_sarcifice_Pio_Christiano_Inv31648.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2250" data-file-width="1900" decoding="async" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Isaac_sarcifice_Pio_Christiano_Inv31648.jpg/220px-Isaac_sarcifice_Pio_Christiano_Inv31648.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Isaac_sarcifice_Pio_Christiano_Inv31648.jpg/330px-Isaac_sarcifice_Pio_Christiano_Inv31648.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Isaac_sarcifice_Pio_Christiano_Inv31648.jpg/440px-Isaac_sarcifice_Pio_Christiano_Inv31648.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Isaac_sarcifice_Pio_Christiano_Inv31648.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>16th-century plaster cast of a late-Roman-era <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacrifice_of_Isaac" title="Sacrifice of Isaac">Sacrifice of Isaac</a>. The hand of God originally came down to restrain Abraham's knife (both are now missing).</div></div></div>
<p>Paintings on the life of Abraham tend to focus on only a few incidents: the sacrifice of Isaac; meeting Melchizedek; entertaining the three angels; Hagar in the desert; and a few others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abeart_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abeart-121">[f]</a></sup> Additionally, Martin O'Kane, a professor of Biblical Studies, writes that the parable of <a href="/wiki/Rich_man_and_Lazarus" title="Rich man and Lazarus">Lazarus</a> resting in the "<a href="/wiki/Bosom_of_Abraham" title="Bosom of Abraham">Bosom of Abraham</a>", as described in the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Luke" title="Gospel of Luke">Gospel of Luke</a>, became an iconic image in Christian works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEExum2007135_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEExum2007135-122">[116]</a></sup> According to O'Kane, artists often chose to divert from the common literary portrayal of Lazarus sitting next to Abraham at a banquet in Heaven and instead focus on the "somewhat incongruous notion of Abraham, the most venerated of patriarchs, holding a naked and vulnerable child in his bosom".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEExum2007135_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEExum2007135-122">[116]</a></sup> Several artists have been inspired by the life of Abraham, including <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer" title="Albrecht Dürer">Albrecht Dürer</a> (1471–1528), <a href="/wiki/Caravaggio" title="Caravaggio">Caravaggio</a> (1573–1610), <a href="/wiki/Donatello" title="Donatello">Donatello</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_van_Dyck" title="Anthony van Dyck">Philip van Dyck</a> (Dutch painter, 1680–1753), and <a href="/wiki/Claude_Lorrain" title="Claude Lorrain">Claude Lorrain</a> (French painter, 1600–1682). <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">Rembrandt</a> (Dutch, 1606–1669) created at least seven works on Abraham, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Paul_Rubens" title="Peter Paul Rubens">Peter Paul Rubens</a> (1577–1640) did several, <a href="/wiki/Marc_Chagall" title="Marc Chagall">Marc Chagall</a> did at least five on Abraham, Gustave Doré (French illustrator, 1832–1883) did six, and <a href="/wiki/James_Tissot" title="James Tissot">James Tissot</a> (French painter and illustrator, 1836–1902) did over twenty works on the subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abeart_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abeart-121">[f]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus_of_Junius_Bassus" title="Sarcophagus of Junius Bassus">Sarcophagus of Junius Bassus</a> depicts a set of biblical stories, including Abraham about to sacrifice Isaac. These sculpted scenes are on the outside of a marble <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Christian_art" title="Early Christian art">Early Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a> used for the burial of <a href="/wiki/Junius_Bassus_Theotecnius" title="Junius Bassus Theotecnius">Junius Bassus</a>. He died in 359. This sarcophagus has been described as "probably the single most famous piece of early Christian relief sculpture."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERutgers1993_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERutgers1993-123">[117]</a></sup> The sarcophagus was originally placed in or under <a href="/wiki/Old_St._Peter%27s_Basilica" title="Old St. Peter's Basilica">Old St. Peter's Basilica</a>, was rediscovered in 1597, and is now below the modern basilica in the Museo Storico del Tesoro della Basilica di San Pietro (Museum of <a href="/wiki/St._Peter%27s_Basilica" title="St. Peter's Basilica">St. Peter's Basilica</a>) in the <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican</a>. The base is approximately 4 ft × 8 ft × 4 ft (1.2 m × 2.4 m × 1.2 m). The Old Testament scenes depicted were chosen as precursors of Christ's sacrifice in the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>, in an early form of <a href="/wiki/Typology_(theology)" title="Typology (theology)">typology</a>. Just to the right of the middle is Daniel in the lion's den and on the left is Abraham about to sacrifice Isaac.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/George_Segal_(artist)" title="George Segal (artist)">George Segal</a> created figural sculptures by molding plastered gauze strips over live models in his 1987 work <i>Abraham's Farewell to Ishmael</i>. The human condition was central to his concerns, and Segal used the Old Testament as a source for his imagery. This sculpture depicts the dilemma faced by Abraham when Sarah demanded that he expel Hagar and Ishmael. In the sculpture, the father's tenderness, Sarah's rage, and Hagar's resigned acceptance portray a range of human emotions. The sculpture was donated to the Miami Art Museum after the artist's death in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_iconography">Christian iconography</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BD%D0%B8_%D0%90%D0%B2%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B0%D0%BC_%D0%B8_%D0%BF%D1%80a%D0%B2%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BD%D0%B8_(%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%98%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8)_%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B7%D0%B1%D0%BE%D1%98%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BA_%D1%83_%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%98%D1%83,_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%BF%D0%B8%D1%81_%D1%83_%D1%81%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%98_%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B5%D1%99%D0%B8_%D0%93%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%87%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B0,_%D0%A1%D1%80%D0%B1%D0%B8%D1%98%D0%B0.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BD%D0%B8_%D0%90%D0%B2%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B0%D0%BC_%D0%B8_%D0%BF%D1%80a%D0%B2%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BD%D0%B8_%28%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%98%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8%29_%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B7%D0%B1%D0%BE%D1%98%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BA_%D1%83_%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%98%D1%83%2C_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%BF%D0%B8%D1%81_%D1%83_%D1%81%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%98_%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B5%D1%99%D0%B8_%D0%93%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%87%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B0%2C_%D0%A1%D1%80%D0%B1%D0%B8%D1%98%D0%B0.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BD%D0%B8_%D0%90%D0%B2%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B0%D0%BC_%D0%B8_%D0%BF%D1%80a%D0%B2%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BD%D0%B8_%28%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%98%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8%29_%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B7%D0%B1%D0%BE%D1%98%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BA_%D1%83_%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%98%D1%83%2C_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%BF%D0%B8%D1%81_%D1%83_%D1%81%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%98_%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B5%D1%99%D0%B8_%D0%93%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%87%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B0%2C_%D0%A1%D1%80%D0%B1%D0%B8%D1%98%D0%B0.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9F%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BD%D0%B8_%D0%90%D0%B2%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B0%D0%BC_%D0%B8_%D0%BF%D1%80a%D0%B2%D0%B5%D0%B4%D0%BD%D0%B8_(%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%98%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8)_%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%B7%D0%B1%D0%BE%D1%98%D0%BD%D0%B8%D0%BA_%D1%83_%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%98%D1%83,_%D0%B6%D0%B8%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%BF%D0%B8%D1%81_%D1%83_%D1%81%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82%D0%BE%D1%98_%D0%BE%D0%B1%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B5%D1%99%D0%B8_%D0%93%D1%80%D0%B0%D1%87%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B8%D1%86%D0%B0,_%D0%A1%D1%80%D0%B1%D0%B8%D1%98%D0%B0.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Abraham in paradise, <a href="/wiki/Gra%C4%8Danica_Monastery" title="Gračanica Monastery">Gračanica Monastery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a></div></div></div>
<p>Usually Abraham can be identified by the context of the image –  the meeting with <a href="/wiki/Melchizedek" title="Melchizedek">Melchizedek</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Abraham_and_three_angels" title="commons:Category:Abraham and three angels">the three visitors</a>, or <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Sacrifice_of_Isaac" title="commons:Category:Sacrifice of Isaac">the sacrifice of Isaac</a>. In solo portraits a sword or knife may be used as his attribute, as in <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:AbrahamMorlaiterJRS.JPG" title="commons:File:AbrahamMorlaiterJRS.JPG">this statue</a> by Gian Maria Morlaiter or <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:AbrahamMonacoJRS.jpg" title="commons:File:AbrahamMonacoJRS.jpg">this painting</a> by Lorenzo Monaco. He always wears a gray or white beard.
</p><p>As early as the beginning of the 3rd century, Christian art followed Christian <a href="/wiki/Typology_(theology)#Sacrifice_of_Isaac" title="Typology (theology)">typology</a> in making the sacrifice of Isaac a foreshadowing of Christ's sacrifice on the cross and its memorial in the sacrifice of the Mass. See for example <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:AltarFuldaClunyJRS.jpg" title="commons:File:AltarFuldaClunyJRS.jpg">this 11th-century Christian altar</a> engraved with Abraham's and other sacrifices taken to prefigure that of Christ in the Eucharist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some early Christian writers interpreted the three visitors as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triune_God" title="Triune God">triune God</a>. Thus in Santa Maria Maggiore, Rome, <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:HospitalityAbrahamSMMaggioreJRS.jpg" title="commons:File:HospitalityAbrahamSMMaggioreJRS.jpg">a 5th-century mosaic</a> portrays only the visitors against a <a href="/wiki/Gold_ground" title="Gold ground">gold ground</a> and puts semitransparent copies of them in the "heavenly" space above the scene. In Eastern Orthodox art, the visit is the chief means by which the Trinity is pictured (<a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Russian_-_Hospitality_of_Abraham_-_Walters_371185.jpg" title="commons:File:Russian - Hospitality of Abraham - Walters 371185.jpg">example</a>). Some images do not include Abraham and Sarah, like Andrei Rublev's <i>Trinity</i>, which shows only the three visitors as beardless youths at a table.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boguslawski_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boguslawski-126">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Fear_and_Trembling" title="Fear and Trembling">Fear and Trembling</a></i> (original <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a> title: <i>Frygt og Bæven</i>) is an influential philosophical work by <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard" title="Søren Kierkegaard">Søren Kierkegaard</a>, published in 1843 under the pseudonym <i>Johannes de silentio</i> (<i>John the Silent</i>). Kierkegaard wanted to understand the anxiety that must have been present in Abraham when God asked him to sacrifice his son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKierkegaard1980155–156_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKierkegaard1980155–156-127">[121]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/W._G._Hardy" title="W. G. Hardy">W. G. Hardy</a>'s novel <i>Father Abraham</i> (1935) tells the fictionalized life story of Abraham.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3>
<p>In 1681, <a href="/wiki/Marc-Antoine_Charpentier" title="Marc-Antoine Charpentier">Marc-Antoine Charpentier</a> released a Dramatic motet (Oratorio), <i>Sacrificim Abrahae</i> H.402 - 402 a - 402 b, for soloists, chorus, doubling instruments and continuo. <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A9bastien_de_Brossard" title="Sébastien de Brossard">Sébastien de Brossard</a> released a cantate Abraham (date unknown).
</p><p>In 1994, <a href="/wiki/Steve_Reich" title="Steve Reich">Steve Reich</a> released an opera named <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cave_(opera)" title="The Cave (opera)">The Cave</a></i>. The title refers to the <a href="/wiki/Cave_of_the_Patriarchs" title="Cave of the Patriarchs">Cave of the Patriarchs</a>. The narrative of the opera is based on the story of Abraham and his immediate family as it is recounted in the various religious texts, and as it is understood by individual people from different cultures and religious traditions.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan" title="Bob Dylan">Bob Dylan</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Highway_61_Revisited_(song)" title="Highway 61 Revisited (song)">Highway 61 Revisited</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[123]</a></sup> is the title track for his 1965 album <i><a href="/wiki/Highway_61_Revisited" title="Highway 61 Revisited">Highway 61 Revisited</a></i>. In 2004, <i><a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">Rolling Stone</a></i> magazine ranked the song as number 364 in their <a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone%27s_500_Greatest_Songs_of_All_Time" title="Rolling Stone's 500 Greatest Songs of All Time">500 Greatest Songs of All Time</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[124]</a></sup> The song has five stanzas. In each stanza, someone describes an unusual problem that is ultimately resolved on Highway 61. In Stanza 1, <a href="/wiki/God_in_Abrahamic_religions" title="God in Abrahamic religions">God</a> tells Abraham to "<a href="/wiki/Binding_of_Isaac" title="Binding of Isaac">kill me a son</a>". God wants the killing done on Highway 61. Abram, the original name of the biblical Abraham, is also the name of Dylan's own father.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">MSS variants: <i>bat Barnebo, bat bar-Nebo, bar-bar-Nebo, bat Karnebi, bat Kar Nebo</i>. Karnebo (<i>outpost of <a href="/wiki/Nabu" title="Nabu">Nabu</a></i>) is attested as a <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> theophoric place-name in <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> inscriptions, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andre_Michaux" title="Andre Michaux">Michaux stone</a>. It referred to at least two separate cities in antiquity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup> Rabbinic tradition connects Karnebo to the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Biblical Hebrew</a> Kar (כר <i>lamb</i>), translating it <i><a href="/wiki/Tumah_and_taharah" title="Tumah and taharah">pure</a> lambs</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-101">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFJeffrey1992">Jeffrey 1992</a>, p. 10 states "St. Augustine, following Paul, regards all Christians as children (or "seed") of Abraham by faith, although "born of strangers" (e.g. In Joan. Ev. 108). St. Ambrose likewise says that by means of their faith Christians possess the promises made to Abraham. Abraham's initial departure from his homeland is understood by St. Caesarius of Arles as a type of Christian leaving the world of carnal habits to follow Christ. Later commentators as diverse as Luther and Kierkegaard recall Abraham as a paradigm of the man of faith. </span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFGrabbe2007">Grabbe, Lester L. (2007).  Williamson, H. G. M (ed.). <a class="external text" href="https://britishacademy.universitypressscholarship.com/view/10.5871/bacad/9780197264010.001.0001/upso-9780197264010-chapter-5" rel="nofollow"><i>Some Recent Issues in the Study of the History of Israel</i></a>. British Academy. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.5871%2Fbacad%2F9780197264010.001.0001" rel="nofollow">10.5871/bacad/9780197264010.001.0001</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-173494-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-173494-6"><bdi>978-0-19-173494-6</bdi></a>. <q>The fact is that we are all minimalists -- at least, when it comes to the patriarchal period and the settlement. When I began my PhD studies more than three decades ago in the USA, the 'substantial historicity' of the patriarchs was widely accepted as was the unified conquest of the land. These days it is quite difficult to find anyone who takes this view.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Some+Recent+Issues+in+the+Study+of+the+History+of+Israel&amp;rft.pub=British+Academy&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.5871%2Fbacad%2F9780197264010.001.0001&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-173494-6&amp;rft.aulast=Grabbe&amp;rft.aufirst=Lester+L.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbritishacademy.universitypressscholarship.com%2Fview%2F10.5871%2Fbacad%2F9780197264010.001.0001%2Fupso-9780197264010-chapter-5&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAbraham"></span></span>

(2020). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=l0gAEAAAQBAJ" rel="nofollow"><i>Covenant Theology: Biblical, Theological, and Historical Perspectives</i></a>. Crossway. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4335-6006-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-4335-6006-4"><bdi>978-1-4335-6006-4</bdi></a>. <q>Paul also shows us how the Abrahamic covenant relates to the covenantal administrations that precede and follow it. ... There is, then, covenantal continuity between the inaugural administration of God's one gracious covenant in the garden of Eden (Gen. 3:15) and the subsequent administration of that covenant to Abraham and his family (Gen. 12; 15; 17). The Abrahamic administration serves to reveal more of the person and work of Christ and, in this way, continue to administer Christ to human beings through faith."</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Covenant+Theology%3A+Biblical%2C+Theological%2C+and+Historical+Perspectives&amp;rft.pub=Crossway&amp;rft.date=2020&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4335-6006-4&amp;rft.aulast=Waters&amp;rft.aufirst=Guy+P.&amp;rft.au=Reid%2C+J.+Nicholas&amp;rft.au=Muether%2C+John+R.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Dl0gAEAAAQBAJ&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAbraham"></span></li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFWilson1989"><a href="/wiki/Marvin_R._Wilson" title="Marvin R. Wilson">Wilson, Marvin R.</a> (1989). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=RPyAG2cduiAC&amp;q=Father+Abraham" rel="nofollow"><i>Our Father Abraham: Jewish Roots of the Christian Faith</i></a>. Massachusetts: Wm. B. Eerdmans Publishing. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8028-0423-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8028-0423-5"><bdi>978-0-8028-0423-5</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Our+Father+Abraham%3A+Jewish+Roots+of+the+Christian+Faith&amp;rft.place=Massachusetts&amp;rft.pub=Wm.+B.+Eerdmans+Publishing&amp;rft.date=1989&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8028-0423-5&amp;rft.aulast=Wilson&amp;rft.aufirst=Marvin+R.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DRPyAG2cduiAC%26q%3DFather%2BAbraham&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAbraham"></span></li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFWright2010"><a href="/wiki/Christopher_J._H._Wright" title="Christopher J. H. Wright">Wright, Christopher J. H.</a> (2010). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=G_2QnrMdwNQC&amp;pg=PA72" rel="nofollow"><i>The Mission of God's People: A Biblical Theology of the Church's Mission</i></a>. Zondervan. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-310-32303-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-310-32303-7"><bdi>978-0-310-32303-7</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Mission+of+God%27s+People%3A+A+Biblical+Theology+of+the+Church%27s+Mission&amp;rft.pub=Zondervan&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-310-32303-7&amp;rft.aulast=Wright&amp;rft.aufirst=Christopher+J.+H.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DG_2QnrMdwNQC%26pg%3DPA72&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAbraham"></span></li></ul>
